فإن قيل : الشرطية ممنوعة وهي قوله : أن لا تكون النعمة نعمة ، إلا إذا كان المنعم عليه محتاجاً إليها مشتهياً لها ، والحاجة والشهوة ضررهما من فعل الله - سبحانه - فتقول : لا نزاع في أنه ما لم يكن الحيوان محتاجاً إلى الشيء ومشتهياً له ، لم يكن إيصال ذلك الشيء إليه نعمة في حقه . لكن لم قلت : إن الحاجة إلى الشيء والشهوة ضرر ؟ وما الدليل على أن الأمر كذلك ؟ سلمنا أنه ضرر ، ولكن لم قلتم : إن ذلك الضرر حاصل بتخليق الله تعالى ؟ وما الدليل عليه ؟ وتقريره : أن كون الإنان محتاجاً إلى الشيء وصف لازم لذاته ولماهيته ، ولما كان الأمر كذلك لم يكن حصوله بسبب جعل الجاهل وفعل الفاعل ، كما أن كون الأربعة زوجاً ، وكون الثلاثة فرداً ، ليس بسبب الجمل والخلق والتكوين . سلمنا أن الحاجة إلى الشيء والشهوة له ، إنما حصل بتخليق الله تعالى وإيجاده . فلم قلتم : إن على هذا التقدير يكون إيصال النعمة إليه عبثاً ؟ وتقريره : أن هذا العبث إنما يلزم إذا قلنا : إنه تعالى لا يوصل إليه النعمة إلا بقدر حاجته وشهوته. أما إذا قلنا : إنه تعالى خلق الحاجة والشهوة فيه . ثم إنه بعد ذلك أعطاه من المنافع ما يزيد على قدر حاجته أضعافاً مضاعفة ، فهذا يمنع من كونه عبثاً . ومثاله : أن من أوصل ديناراً من الغير إلى الإنسان ، ثم أعطاه ألف دينار ، فإنه لا يقال : إنه عابث في فعله ، بل يقال : إنه منعم عليه ، فكذا ههنا ( والله أعلم )

والجواب : قوله : ما الدليل على أن الحاجة والشهوة ضرر ؟ قلنا : هذه المقدمة معلومة الصحة بالضرورة . فإن كل أحد يكره كونه محتاجاً إلى الشيء ، ويحب كونه غنياً عنه . ومن أنكر هذه المقدمة ، فقد نازع في البديهيات . ولذلك قال بعضهم : غنيت بلا مال عن الناس كلهم وإن الغنى الأعلى : عن الشيء ، لا به ومن الأمثال المشهورة : ( إن استغناءك عن الشيء ، أعظم من استغنالك بالشيء ) وأيضاً : قد اتفق العقلاء على أنه يجب تنزيه الله عن الشهوة وعن الحاجة. وحكموا بأنه إنما يجب تنزيه الله عنهما لكونهما من باب النقائص والآفات. وأيضاً : إن المحتاج إلى الشيء إن لم يجد المحتاج إليه ، كان الألم حاصلاً ، وإن وجده إلا أنه يتوقف كمال حاله على وجدان ذلك الشيء . فالتوقف على الغير يوجب الإمكان والحدوث. وكل ذلك من باب النقائص والمضار وأيضاً : أما قوله : لم قلتم إن الحاجة إلى الشيء حصلت بتخليق الله ؟ قلنا : الجواب عنه من وجوه : الأول : إنا نجد إنسانين أحدهما شديد الرغبة في الملاهي والمشاهي عظيم الميل إليهما ، لا يمكنه المصابرة عنهما إلا بتكلف شديد ، وجهد عظيم . ويكون الثاني شديد النفرة عنهما ، عظيم الانقباض عنها بحسب الطبع ( حتى أنه لا تخطر هذه الأمور بباله في أكثر العمر، فلو خطر شيء منها بباله ، فإن
He seizes a dinar from a person, then gives him another dinar. And on this assumption, that would not be a favor, but rather pure futility. Thus it is established that if rational ugliness were considered in the right of God Most High, it would be necessary to be certain that He Most High is not a benefactor to any of His servants; rather it would be necessary that He be acting in vain in everything He did and created. And since the people of religions have agreed that He Most High is wise in His action, a benefactor to His servants, we know that the judgment of reason concerning God Most High regarding good and ugliness is false (and God knows best).

If it is said: The conditional statement is denied, namely his statement: that a favor is not a favor unless the one favored is in need of it and desires it, and need and desire are harms from the action of God—glory be to Him—so you say: There is no dispute that as long as the animal is not in need of something and does not desire it, delivering that thing to him is not a favor regarding him. But why did you say that need for something and desire are harm? And what is the evidence that it is so? We concede that it is harm, but why did you say that that harm occurs by the creation of God Most High? And what is the evidence for it? And its exposition: that a person's being in need of something is an attribute necessary to his essence and quiddity; and since it is so, its occurrence is not due to the act of the ignorant or the action of an agent, just as four being even and three being odd is not due to creation and formation. We concede that need for something and desire for it occur only by the creation and bringing into existence of God Most High. So why did you say that on this assumption, delivering the favor to him is futile? And its exposition: that this futility only follows if we say that He Most High only delivers the favor to him according to the measure of his need and desire. But if we say that He Most High created need and desire in him, then after that He gave him from benefits what exceeds the measure of his need many times over, then this prevents it from being futile. And its example: that one who delivers a dinar from another to a person, then gives him a thousand dinars, it is not said that he is acting in vain in his action; rather it is said that he is a benefactor to him. Thus is it here (and God knows best).

And the answer: His statement: What is the evidence that need and desire are harm? We say: This premise is known to be true by necessity. For everyone hates being in need of something and loves being independent of it. And whoever denies this premise has disputed matters of primary intuition. Therefore one said: I am independent without wealth from all people, and the highest independence is independence from a thing, not by it. And among the famous proverbs: Your independence from a thing is greater than your independence by a thing. Also: The rational have agreed that it is necessary to declare God free from desire and from need. And they have judged that it is necessary to declare God free from them because they are of the category of deficiencies and defects. Also: One in need of something, if he does not find the thing he needs, pain occurs; and if he finds it, yet his perfection depends on finding that thing. So dependence on another necessitates contingency and origination. And all that is of the category of deficiencies and harms. Also: As for his statement: Why did you say that need for something occurred by the creation of God? We say: The answer to it is from several aspects: First: We find two persons, one of them intensely desiring amusements and pleasures, with great inclination towards them, unable to abstain from them except with great effort and immense exertion. And the second is intensely averse to them, with great natural repulsion from them, such that these matters do not occur to his mind in most of his life; if something of them occurs to him, then
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage is a continuous theological argument consisting of: (1) a proof that rational judgments of good and evil do not apply to God, (2) an objection against this proof, and (3) an answer to the objection.

The author begins by presenting a scenario: if someone extorts a dinar from a person and then gives it back, that is not a favor but mere futility. He then generalizes: if rational ugliness (al-qubh al-'aqli) were valid regarding God, then we would have to conclude that God is not a benefactor to any of His creatures, and that He acts in vain in everything. But all religious communities agree that God is wise and a benefactor. Therefore, the judgment of reason assigning good and evil to God is false.

The objector then raises several points. First, the conditional premise is denied: a favor is only a favor if the recipient needs and desires it; need and desire are harms from God. The objector concedes that without need and desire, a gift is not a favor. But he questions whether need and desire are indeed harms, and even if they are, whether they are created by God. He argues that need is an essential attribute of the creature, not caused by any agent, like the evenness of four or oddness of three. Even if need is created, the objector denies that delivering benefits becomes futile, because God gives far more than the need, similar to someone giving a thousand dinars after one: that is generosity, not futility.

In the answer, the author first addresses the claim that need and desire are not harms. He asserts that this premise is self-evident (ma'luma bi al-darura): everyone hates being in need and loves independence. Denying this is to deny primary intuitions. He cites a saying and a proverb to support this. Second, he argues that rational people agree that God must be declared free from need and desire because they are defects. Third, need implies dependency, which leads to contingency and origination, which are deficiencies. Finally, regarding the creation of need in individuals, the author gives an example of two people with opposite natural inclinations—one strongly attracted to pleasures and one repelled—showing that such dispositions are created by God, not essential. The argument cuts off at this point.

Key Terms
القبح العقلي
العبث
الحكيم
المنعم

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The fact that humans perceive good and evil innately.
D) The example of a person giving a gift.

Key Takeaway
The application of rational moral categories to God leads to the absurd conclusion that God is a futile actor, contradicting the consensus of all religions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a loss that feels meaningless, do you truly believe God is still the Wise and Beneficent, or do you let your own sense of fairness accuse Him?
?
The passage says that if rational ugliness applied to God, He would not be a benefactor. Have you ever doubted God's goodness because something seemed unfair in your eyes? What would it take to surrender that judgment?
?
If you deeply internalized that God's actions are not subject to your rational categories, what fear or resentment in your relationship with Him would you have to let go of, starting today?

Objection and Refutation: Are Need and Desire Harms Caused by God?
Objection and partial refutation regarding the harmfulness of need and desire.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection raised against the proof and the author's initial response, which argues that need and desire are self-evidently harms and that they are created by God.

Core Idea
Need and desire are self-evident harms, and their creation by God does not make His beneficence futile.

The objector challenges the proof by questioning whether need and desire are harms, and even if they are, whether they are created by God. The author responds by asserting the self-evident nature of these harms and providing arguments, including examples of divergent human dispositions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objector denies the conditional premise and raises three sub-questions. Read through the objection carefully.
The objection is detailed, so pay attention to each point.
2
Analyze the author's first response
The author first addresses whether need and desire are harms, arguing it is self-evident and supported by proverbial wisdom.
3
Analyze the author's second response
The author addresses whether need is created by God, citing the example of two people with opposite natural inclinations.
This response is cut off.

Source Text

يغصب من إنسان ديناراً ( ثم يعطيه ديناراً ) آخر . وإنه على هذا التقدير لا يكون ذلك إنعاماً ، بل يكون محض العبث ، فيثبت أن القبح العقلي لو كان معتبراً في حق الله تعالى ، لوجب القطع بأنه تعالى غير منعم على أحد من عبيده ، بل كان يلزم أن يكون عابئاً في كل ما فعل وخلق ، ولما أجمع أهل الملل على أنه تعالى حكيم في فعله ، منعم على عبيده ، علمنا أن حكم العقل على الله تعالى بالحسن والقبح باطل ( والله أعلم )

فإن قيل : الشرطية ممنوعة وهي قوله : أن لا تكون النعمة نعمة ، إلا إذا كان المنعم عليه محتاجاً إليها مشتهياً لها ، والحاجة والشهوة ضررهما من فعل الله - سبحانه - فتقول : لا نزاع في أنه ما لم يكن الحيوان محتاجاً إلى الشيء ومشتهياً له ، لم يكن إيصال ذلك الشيء إليه نعمة في حقه . لكن لم قلت : إن الحاجة إلى الشيء والشهوة ضرر ؟ وما الدليل على أن الأمر كذلك ؟ سلمنا أنه ضرر ، ولكن لم قلتم : إن ذلك الضرر حاصل بتخليق الله تعالى ؟ وما الدليل عليه ؟ وتقريره : أن كون الإنان محتاجاً إلى الشيء وصف لازم لذاته ولماهيته ، ولما كان الأمر كذلك لم يكن حصوله بسبب جعل الجاهل وفعل الفاعل ، كما أن كون الأربعة زوجاً ، وكون الثلاثة فرداً ، ليس بسبب الجمل والخلق والتكوين . سلمنا أن الحاجة إلى الشيء والشهوة له ، إنما حصل بتخليق الله تعالى وإيجاده . فلم قلتم : إن على هذا التقدير يكون إيصال النعمة إليه عبثاً ؟ وتقريره : أن هذا العبث إنما يلزم إذا قلنا : إنه تعالى لا يوصل إليه النعمة إلا بقدر حاجته وشهوته. أما إذا قلنا : إنه تعالى خلق الحاجة والشهوة فيه . ثم إنه بعد ذلك أعطاه من المنافع ما يزيد على قدر حاجته أضعافاً مضاعفة ، فهذا يمنع من كونه عبثاً . ومثاله : أن من أوصل ديناراً من الغير إلى الإنسان ، ثم أعطاه ألف دينار ، فإنه لا يقال : إنه عابث في فعله ، بل يقال : إنه منعم عليه ، فكذا ههنا ( والله أعلم )

والجواب : قوله : ما الدليل على أن الحاجة والشهوة ضرر ؟ قلنا : هذه المقدمة معلومة الصحة بالضرورة . فإن كل أحد يكره كونه محتاجاً إلى الشيء ، ويحب كونه غنياً عنه . ومن أنكر هذه المقدمة ، فقد نازع في البديهيات . ولذلك قال بعضهم : غنيت بلا مال عن الناس كلهم وإن الغنى الأعلى : عن الشيء ، لا به ومن الأمثال المشهورة : ( إن استغناءك عن الشيء ، أعظم من استغنالك بالشيء ) وأيضاً : قد اتفق العقلاء على أنه يجب تنزيه الله عن الشهوة وعن الحاجة. وحكموا بأنه إنما يجب تنزيه الله عنهما لكونهما من باب النقائص والآفات. وأيضاً : إن المحتاج إلى الشيء إن لم يجد المحتاج إليه ، كان الألم حاصلاً ، وإن وجده إلا أنه يتوقف كمال حاله على وجدان ذلك الشيء . فالتوقف على الغير يوجب الإمكان والحدوث. وكل ذلك من باب النقائص والمضار وأيضاً : أما قوله : لم قلتم إن الحاجة إلى الشيء حصلت بتخليق الله ؟ قلنا : الجواب عنه من وجوه : الأول : إنا نجد إنسانين أحدهما شديد الرغبة في الملاهي والمشاهي عظيم الميل إليهما ، لا يمكنه المصابرة عنهما إلا بتكلف شديد ، وجهد عظيم . ويكون الثاني شديد النفرة عنهما ، عظيم الانقباض عنها بحسب الطبع ( حتى أنه لا تخطر هذه الأمور بباله في أكثر العمر، فلو خطر شيء منها بباله ، فإن
He seizes a dinar from a person, then gives him another dinar. And on this assumption, that would not be a favor, but rather pure futility. Thus it is established that if rational ugliness were considered in the right of God Most High, it would be necessary to be certain that He Most High is not a benefactor to any of His servants; rather it would be necessary that He be acting in vain in everything He did and created. And since the people of religions have agreed that He Most High is wise in His action, a benefactor to His servants, we know that the judgment of reason concerning God Most High regarding good and ugliness is false (and God knows best).

If it is said: The conditional statement is denied, namely his statement: that a favor is not a favor unless the one favored is in need of it and desires it, and need and desire are harms from the action of God—glory be to Him—so you say: There is no dispute that as long as the animal is not in need of something and does not desire it, delivering that thing to him is not a favor regarding him. But why did you say that need for something and desire are harm? And what is the evidence that it is so? We concede that it is harm, but why did you say that that harm occurs by the creation of God Most High? And what is the evidence for it? And its exposition: that a person's being in need of something is an attribute necessary to his essence and quiddity; and since it is so, its occurrence is not due to the act of the ignorant or the action of an agent, just as four being even and three being odd is not due to creation and formation. We concede that need for something and desire for it occur only by the creation and bringing into existence of God Most High. So why did you say that on this assumption, delivering the favor to him is futile? And its exposition: that this futility only follows if we say that He Most High only delivers the favor to him according to the measure of his need and desire. But if we say that He Most High created need and desire in him, then after that He gave him from benefits what exceeds the measure of his need many times over, then this prevents it from being futile. And its example: that one who delivers a dinar from another to a person, then gives him a thousand dinars, it is not said that he is acting in vain in his action; rather it is said that he is a benefactor to him. Thus is it here (and God knows best).

And the answer: His statement: What is the evidence that need and desire are harm? We say: This premise is known to be true by necessity. For everyone hates being in need of something and loves being independent of it. And whoever denies this premise has disputed matters of primary intuition. Therefore one said: I am independent without wealth from all people, and the highest independence is independence from a thing, not by it. And among the famous proverbs: Your independence from a thing is greater than your independence by a thing. Also: The rational have agreed that it is necessary to declare God free from desire and from need. And they have judged that it is necessary to declare God free from them because they are of the category of deficiencies and defects. Also: One in need of something, if he does not find the thing he needs, pain occurs; and if he finds it, yet his perfection depends on finding that thing. So dependence on another necessitates contingency and origination. And all that is of the category of deficiencies and harms. Also: As for his statement: Why did you say that need for something occurred by the creation of God? We say: The answer to it is from several aspects: First: We find two persons, one of them intensely desiring amusements and pleasures, with great inclination towards them, unable to abstain from them except with great effort and immense exertion. And the second is intensely averse to them, with great natural repulsion from them, such that these matters do not occur to his mind in most of his life; if something of them occurs to him, then
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage is a continuous theological argument consisting of: (1) a proof that rational judgments of good and evil do not apply to God, (2) an objection against this proof, and (3) an answer to the objection.

The author begins by presenting a scenario: if someone extorts a dinar from a person and then gives it back, that is not a favor but mere futility. He then generalizes: if rational ugliness (al-qubh al-'aqli) were valid regarding God, then we would have to conclude that God is not a benefactor to any of His creatures, and that He acts in vain in everything. But all religious communities agree that God is wise and a benefactor. Therefore, the judgment of reason assigning good and evil to God is false.

The objector then raises several points. First, the conditional premise is denied: a favor is only a favor if the recipient needs and desires it; need and desire are harms from God. The objector concedes that without need and desire, a gift is not a favor. But he questions whether need and desire are indeed harms, and even if they are, whether they are created by God. He argues that need is an essential attribute of the creature, not caused by any agent, like the evenness of four or oddness of three. Even if need is created, the objector denies that delivering benefits becomes futile, because God gives far more than the need, similar to someone giving a thousand dinars after one: that is generosity, not futility.

In the answer, the author first addresses the claim that need and desire are not harms. He asserts that this premise is self-evident (ma'luma bi al-darura): everyone hates being in need and loves independence. Denying this is to deny primary intuitions. He cites a saying and a proverb to support this. Second, he argues that rational people agree that God must be declared free from need and desire because they are defects. Third, need implies dependency, which leads to contingency and origination, which are deficiencies. Finally, regarding the creation of need in individuals, the author gives an example of two people with opposite natural inclinations—one strongly attracted to pleasures and one repelled—showing that such dispositions are created by God, not essential. The argument cuts off at this point.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the author's argument depends on denying that need is a harm, but the author insists it is self-evident.
Correction: The author explicitly states that the harm of need is known by necessity and anyone denying it has disputed primary intuitions.

Checkpoint

A) Philosophical arguments about dependency.
B) The consensus of rational people and self-evident intuition.
C) The fact that need leads to pain if unsatisfied.
D) Both B and C.

Key Takeaway
The author defends the harmfulness of need and desire as self-evident and argues that they are created by God, but the argument is incomplete.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a strong desire for something, do you see it as a harm from Allah or a natural part of being human? How does the author's argument that everyone hates being in need challenge your attitude toward your own desires?
?
The text says that rational people agree God must be free from need and desire because they are defects. If need and desire are defects, what does that mean for your own state of being constantly in need of Allah? Are you comfortable with that dependence, or do you resent it?
?
The objector suggests that if need is created by God, then fulfilling that need is not a favor. How does the author's response—that God gives far beyond what is needed—change your perspective on the blessings you receive daily? Do you truly see them as overwhelming generosity or merely as fulfillment of obligations?

The Animal Analogy: Why Reason Cannot Judge Divine Actions
The author proves that divine actions are not subject to human moral judgments using an analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why human reason's judgments of good and evil do not apply to God's actions, as demonstrated through the animal analogy.

Core Idea
A favor requires need and desire; without them, even a gift is not a blessing.

This passage presents a powerful analogy to illustrate a key point in Islamic theology: that human moral categories cannot be applied to God. By examining a simple example—throwing a pearl necklace to a dog and a bone to a human—the author shows that something is only a true blessing if the recipient desires and needs it. He then applies this insight to divine actions, concluding that God's actions transcend our notions of good and evil.

Guided Path
1
Read the analogy and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the overall flow. Notice the example of the dog and the human.
The analogy is central to the argument; pay attention to the reversal of the gifts.
2
Extract the condition for a blessing
Identify why throwing a necklace to a dog is not a blessing, but throwing a bone to a dog is. The key is desire and need.
The author uses the terms 'shahwa' (desire) and 'haja' (need) to establish the condition.
3
Apply the condition to divine actions
Consider how the same logic applies to God: if He creates a being with a desire and then fulfills it, the benefit and harm cancel out, making the agent seem playful.
This leads to the conclusion that God cannot be judged by human standards of good and evil.
4
Understand the conclusion
The author concludes that reason's judgment (tahsin wa taqbiḥ) is invalid for divine actions, as proven by the absurdity of calling God 'playful' (ʿābiṯ).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كذلك لم يكن إيصال مثل هذا الشيء إلى الحيوان إنعاماً في حقه ، ومثال هذا : أنك إذا رميت قلادة من الدر إلى كلب ، ورميت عظماً إلى إنسان لم يكن ذلك إنعاماً في حق كل واحد منهما ، لأن الكلب لا حاجة له إلى وجدان القلادة من الدر ، ولا شهوة له إلى وجدانها ، وكذلك الإنسان لا تتعلق حاجته بوجدان ذلك العظم ولا تتعلق شهوته بتحصيله ، أما إذا قلبت الأمر ورميت القلادة من الدر إلى الإنسان ورميت العظم إلى الكلب ، كان ذلك إنعاماً في حق الإنسان وفي حق الكلب ، لأن الإنسان يشتهي وجدان القلادة من الدر، والكلب يشتهي تناول العظم. فيثبت أن الحيوان ما لم يكن مشتهياً للشيء ومحتاجاً إليه لم يكن إيصاله إليه إنعاماً في حقه ، وإذا ثبت هذا ظهر أن تلك الزيادة ، يمتنع كونها نعمة ، إلا إذا كان المنعم عليه مشتهياً لها ، وراغباً في تحصيلها ، ومحتاجاً إلى الفوز بها ، ومتى كان الأمر كذلك ، فحينئذ يعود ما ذكرنا من أنه يصير قدر النعمة الحاصلة في الحال، مساوياً لقدر المضرة التي كانت حاصلة في الزمان الماضي، بسبب خلق الشهوة والحاجة فيه ، وحينئذ تتقابل المنفعة بالمضرة . والألم باللذة ، والخير بالشر. ومثل هذا الفاعل يسمى عابثاً بمقتضى تحسين العقل وتقبيحه ، وغير منعم البتة ، ولما كان ذلك باطلا بإجماع العقلاء علمنا أن حكم العقل على أفعال الله بالتحسين والتقبيح باطل ( غير ملتفت إليه . والله أعلم) (۱)
Similarly, delivering such a thing to an animal is not a favor (ni'mah) to it. An example of this: if you throw a pearl necklace to a dog, and throw a bone to a human, that is not a favor to either of them, because the dog has no need for finding the pearl necklace, nor any desire for finding it; likewise, the human's need is not connected to finding that bone, nor is his desire connected to obtaining it. But if you reverse the matter and throw the pearl necklace to the human and throw the bone to the dog, that is a favor to the human and to the dog, because the human desires to find the pearl necklace, and the dog desires to eat the bone. So it is established that as long as an animal does not desire a thing and is not in need of it, delivering it to him is not a favor to him. And when this is established, it becomes clear that that additional [blessing] is impossible to be a blessing (ni'mah), except if the one upon whom the blessing is bestowed desires it, longs for its attainment, and needs to obtain it. And whenever the matter is so, then what we mentioned returns: that the measure of the blessing obtained in the present becomes equal to the measure of the harm that was present in the past time, due to the creation of desire and need in him. Then benefit and harm, pain and pleasure, and good and evil become balanced. Such an agent is called a 'playful one' (ʿābiṯ) according to reason's judgment of good and evil (taḥsīn al-ʿaql wa-taqbīḥihi), and is not a benefactor at all. And since that is false by consensus of the rational people, we know that reason's judgment upon the actions of God by way of good and evil is invalid, not to be heeded. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage is a proof that human reason's judgment of good and evil (taḥsīn wa-taqbīḥ) cannot be applied to God's actions. The author uses an analogy to establish the conditions for an act to be considered a genuine favor (ni'mah). He first gives an example: throwing a pearl necklace to a dog and a bone to a human. The dog has no desire or need for a necklace, so it is not a favor to him; similarly, the human has no desire for a bone. But if you reverse it, giving the necklace to the human and the bone to the dog, it becomes a favor because each desires what is fitting. From this, the author concludes that a thing is only a blessing if the recipient desires and needs it. Then he applies this to God's actions: if God were to create a creature with a desire and need and then fulfill it, the pleasure and harm would cancel out, making the agent a 'playful one' (ʿābiṯ) according to human reason—which is absurd. Therefore, reason's moral categories do not apply to God. The argument concludes that since rational people unanimously reject such a notion, it is false to judge God's actions by human standards.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's blessings are measured by human standards of need and desire.
Correction: The author argues that since God creates need and desire, any supposed balance of benefit and harm would render God capricious, which is impossible; thus human moral categories do not apply to divine actions.

Checkpoint
What does the animal analogy demonstrate about the nature of a true blessing (ni'mah)?
A) It depends on the value of the object given.
B) It requires the recipient to desire and need the object.
C) It is determined by the giver's intention.
D) It is relative to the species of the recipient.

Key Takeaway
Human reason's moral judgments do not apply to Allah's actions because what is considered a favor depends on the recipient's need and desire, which are themselves created by Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a blessing you received today. Did you feel entitled to it because you 'needed' it? How does this lesson's claim—that even your need is created by Allah—challenge that sense of entitlement? What will you say to Allah tonight in gratitude?
?
When you face a trial after a period of ease, do you ever find yourself thinking that Allah is being unfair? The lesson shows that if we apply human reason, any act of giving would cancel out the prior harm, making the giver 'playful'. Can you identify a specific complaint you have held against Allah, and will you now reject it as a false judgment of your limited reason?
?
If you truly accepted that Allah's actions are beyond human categories of 'good' and 'evil', what would change about how you react to the next difficulty in your life? Name one concrete response you will commit to—such as remaining silent, or saying 'alhamdulillah' before you understand.

Refuting the Idea That Times Have Inherent Benefits
Author refutes inherent benefits in times via tripartite division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that specific times have inherent benefits is rationally untenable and how this refutes the Mu'tazili doctrine of rational good and evil (al-tahsin wa al-taqbih) regarding God's actions.

Core Idea
If times had inherent benefits, God would be necessitated, not free.

In this passage, the author tackles a key Mu'tazili argument that times can be inherently beneficial or harmful. He systematically examines the possible ways a time could be specified for a benefit and shows each leads to absurdity. The argument ultimately demonstrates that rational judgments of good and evil cannot be applied to God, preserving divine free choice.

Guided Path
1
Understand the tripartite division
The author divides the possibilities of how a time could be specified for a benefit: either by its essence, by a necessary concomitant, or by neither. Recognize this structure.
This division is exhaustive and covers all logical possibilities.
2
Refutation of the first division
Read the three proofs against the first possibility. Note how each proof undermines the idea that a time's essence can cause a benefit.
The proofs rely on the contingency of time and the impossibility of intrinsic properties in non-existence.
3
Apply the same refutation to the second division
The author states that the same three proofs refute the second possibility. Understand why the reasoning is identical.
4
Analyze the third division and its consequence
If neither essence nor concomitant, then a free chooser must specify the time. This leads to an infinite regress unless one admits that God acts without inherent reasons, which would be 'futility' (al-'abath).
The author uses this to argue that rational morality would force God to act for reasons, negating free choice.
5
Grasp the conclusion and its universal application
The author concludes that the doctrine of rational good and evil is false regarding God, as it would imply God is a necessitating agent and the world eternal. He also notes the proof applies to every event and attribute.
This is a key argument in Ash'ari theology against Mu'tazili rationalism.

اختصاص ذلك الوقت المعين بتلك المصلحة المعينة لذات ذلك الوقت ولعينه . فنقول : هذا باطل لوجوه : أحدها : إن هذه الأوقات عند القائلين بحدوث العالم ، كانت قبل حدوث العالم نفي محض وعدم صرف ، والعدم المحض يمتنع أن يخالف بعضه بعضاً بالخواص الذاتية ، والآثار اللازمة. وثانيها : أن نقول : هذه الأوقات المختلفة ، والآثار المختلفة إما أن تكون واجبة الوجود لذواتها أو لا تكون كذلك ، والأول يقتضي امتناع العلم عليها ، فيكون الوقت السابق هو بعينه الوقت اللاحق، وذلك باطل بالضرورة، والثاني يقتضي أن تكون هذه الأوقات والأثار محدثة متعاقبة متلاحقة ، وحينئذ يعود البحث في أنه لم يوجد ذلك الوقت المشتمل على تلك المصلحة في ذلك الوقت ، ولم يحدث قبله ولا بعده ؟ وثالثها : أنه لو كان الوقت المعين له صلاحية أن يقتضي لذاته ، كونه منشئاً لتلك المصلحة المعينة ، فلم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن الوقت المعين يقتضي لذاته حدوث العالم ؟ فإن ذلك الوقت المعين لما صح أن يكون مؤثراً في الآثار ، لم يمتنع أيضاً كونه مؤثراً في سائر الحوادث ، وحينئذ لا يمكننا أن نقطع بأن المؤثر في حدوث العالم، ليس هو ذلك الوقت المعين ، وأي دليل يذكر في إبطال هذا فإن يصير منتقضاً بقولهم : إن ذلك الوقت اقتضى حصول تلك الخاصية لذاته ، فيثبت بهذه الوجوه الثلاثة : فساد قول من يقول : إن ذلك الوقت المعين، اقتضى لذاته حصول تلك المصلحة . والقسم الثاني : وهو أن المقتضي لحصول تلك المصلحة لازم من لوازم ذلك الوقت ، فهذا أيضاً باطل ، لعين تلك الدلائل الثلاثة . وأما القسم الثالث : وهو أن ذلك الوقت اختص بتلك الخاصية لا لذاته ، ولا لشيء من لوازم ذاته ، فحينئذ وجب القطع بأن الفاعل المختار اختص ذلك الوقت بتلك الخاصية المعينة ، وإن كان ذلك الخاصية أخرى عاد التقسيم الأول فيه ، ولزم التسلسل ، وهو محال ، فيثبت أن بتقدير أن يكون العالم محدثاً فإنه يجب الاعتراف بأن الفاعل المختار خصص إحداثه بذلك الوقت المعين من غير رعاية حكمة ولا مصلحة ، فيكون ذلك محض العبث ، وهو قبيح في العقول ، فيثبت بهذا أن حكم العقل في التحسين والتقبيح لو كان معتبراً ، لزم القول بقدم العالم، ويكون الإله موجباً بالذات ، لا فاعلا مختاراً ، لكنا بينا : أن القول بالتحسين والتقبيح ، فرع على القول بإثبات الفاعل المختار ، وكل فرع أوجب فساد الأصل كان باطلاً ، فوجب أن يكون القول بالتحسين والتقبيح باطلاً في أحكام الله . واعلم . أن هذا الدليل الذي ذكرناه عائد في حدوث كل واحد من الحوادث في وقته المعين ، وفي اختصاص كل واحدة من الذوات بصفته المعينة ، وبمقداره المعين، وبحيزه المعين . فإنا نقول : اختصاصه بتلك الخاصية إما أن يكون الخاصية في ذلك الوقت ، أو لشيء من لوازم ذلك الوقت ، أو لشيء من عوارض ذلك الوقت ، وحينئذ يعود التقسيم بتمامه . وهذا الدليل في الحقيقة ليس دليلاً واحداً ، بل هو دلائل لا نهاية لها بحسب حدوث كل حادث يحدث إلى الأبد الذي لا آخر له .
That it is not the case, and the first is false. That is because the specification of that time with that particular benefit is either for its essence, or for the necessities of its essence, or for neither of these two divisions, and the three divisions are false. As for the first: that the specification of that particular time with that particular benefit is for the essence of that time and its identity. We say: this is false for reasons: One of them: that these times, according to those who affirm the origination of the world, were before the origination of the world pure negation and absolute non-existence, and absolute non-existence cannot differ in part from part in essential properties and necessary effects. The second: that we say: these different times and different effects are either necessary of existence in themselves or not, and the first entails the impossibility of [؟] knowledge of them, so the previous time would be identical to the subsequent time, and that is necessarily false, and the second entails that these times and effects are originated, successive, sequential, and then the inquiry returns as to why that time containing that benefit existed at that time and did not occur before or after? The third: that if the particular time had the capacity to necessitate in itself its being the source of that particular benefit, then why is it not permissible to say that the particular time necessitates in itself the origination of the world? For since that particular time can be effective in [producing] effects, it is not impossible that it also be effective in other events, and then we cannot be certain that the effective agent in the origination of the world is not that particular time, and any proof mentioned to invalidate this would be refuted by their saying: that time necessitated the occurrence of that property in itself. So by these three proofs the falsity of the statement of one who says that that particular time necessitated in itself the occurrence of that benefit is established. The second division: that the necessitator of the occurrence of that benefit is a necessary concomitant of that time, this is also false due to the same three proofs. As for the third division: that that time was specified with that property neither for its essence nor for anything from the necessary concomitants of its essence, then it is necessary to judge that the free chooser specified that time with that particular property, and if that property were another, the first division would return in it, and infinite regress would follow, which is impossible. So it is established that, assuming the world is originated, it is necessary to acknowledge that the free chooser specified its origination in that particular time without regard for wisdom or benefit, so that would be pure futility, and that is ugly in the intellects. So by this it is established that if the judgment of reason concerning good (tahsin) and evil (taqbih) were considered valid, the statement that the world is eternal would be necessary, and the deity would be a necessitating agent (mujib bi al-dhat), not a free chooser (fa'il mukhtar). But we have shown that the statement concerning good and evil (al-tahsin wa al-taqbih) is a branch of the affirmation of the free chooser, and every branch that necessitates the corruption of the root is false. So it is necessary that the statement concerning good and evil is false regarding the judgments of God. And know that this proof we have mentioned applies to the origination of every single event in its particular time, and to the specification of every entity with its particular attribute, its particular measure, and its particular position. For we say: its specification with that property is either from the property in that time, or from something from the necessary concomitants of that time, or from something from the accidents of that time, and then the entire division returns. And this proof in reality is not one proof, but rather proofs without end according to the origination of every event that occurs forever without end.
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage is a complex argument refuting the idea that specific times have inherent properties that cause benefits. The author begins by stating that the specification of a time for a particular benefit must fall into one of three divisions: (1) the benefit is inherent in the time's essence, (2) the benefit is a necessary concomitant of the time, or (3) neither. He then argues all three are false. For the first division, he gives three proofs: First, before the world's creation, times were absolute non-existence, and non-existence cannot have essential differences. Second, times are either necessary in themselves (leading to identical times, absurd) or contingent and originated, which then requires explanation of why the benefit occurred at that specific moment. Third, if a time could necessitate a specific benefit, it could also necessitate the world's creation, making it impossible to deny that time is the creator. The second division is rejected by the same three proofs. The third division forces the conclusion that a free chooser (God) must have specified the time without any inherent reason, which would be pure futility and is considered ugly by reason. This leads to the main conclusion: if rational judgments of good and evil were valid, then God would be a necessitating agent (not free) and the world would be eternal. But since the free chooser is the root of moral judgments, the branch (moral judgments) cannot invalidate the root. Therefore, moral judgments are invalid regarding God's actions. The author then notes that this proof applies to every event, attribute, measure, and position, and is in fact an endless series of proofs.

Key Terms
المصلحة
التحسين والتقبيح
الفاعل المختار
الإله الموجب بالذات
العبث
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author denies any purpose in God's actions, implying God acts arbitrarily.
Correction: The author is actually showing that Mu'tazili rationalism would force God to act for reasons, negating free choice. The Ash'ari position is that God acts with wisdom, but humans cannot judge His actions by rational standards of good and evil.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what would be the consequence if rational judgments of good and evil were considered valid?
A) It would prove the world's temporal origination.
B) It would necessitate the world's eternity and God being a necessitating agent.
C) It would establish God's wisdom and purpose in creation.
D) It would prove that times have inherent benefits.

Key Takeaway
The argument demonstrates that rational judgments of good and evil cannot be applied to God, because they would undermine His free choice and lead to absurdities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you anxiously wait for a 'right time' in your life—be it for marriage, a job, or a difficult decision—do you unconsciously believe that some moments are inherently blessed or cursed? How does this lesson expose that belief as a subtle denial of God's sovereign freedom, and what step can you take today to entrust the timing of every outcome solely to Him?
?
You have probably demanded that God explain the 'benefit' behind a painful trial. But if this proof is true, then your demand rests on the assumption that God must act according to your understanding of good and evil. Are you willing to accept that God's specification of your trials is not subject to your rational approval? What would it cost you to surrender that demand and simply trust His choice?
?
The proof argues that if rational good and evil applied to God, He would be forced into necessity, not free choice. Look at your own prayer life: When you ask for something, do you believe God is morally obligated to answer in a certain way? If so, are you inadvertently treating God as a necessitating agent rather than a free chooser? How can you reshape your prayers to reflect trust in His absolute freedom?

Rational Judgments

The Reductio Against God's Obligation
A reductio argument against rational judgments for God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the fourth argument uses a reductio ad absurdum to reject the validity of rational judgments of good and evil in relation to God.

Core Idea
If rational judgments were valid, they would oblige God—which is impossible.

This lesson presents a concise but powerful argument: if human reason could independently judge good and evil, then God would be bound to command certain duties—a conclusion the author deems absurd. By rejecting the consequence, the premise is also rejected. Let's examine the logic step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the compact argument
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author states the conditional premise and then denies the conclusion.
The argument is only one sentence in Arabic—dense but complete.
2
Identify the conditional structure
The argument has an 'if... then...' form. Identify the antecedent, the consequent, and the final negation.
The consequent is: it would be valid for God to oblige knowledge, praise, and gratitude.
3
Understand why the consequent is rejected
The author says the consequent is 'batil' (invalid). Consider why it might be impossible for God to have such obligations.
This hinges on divine transcendence—God cannot be constrained by human reason.
4
Apply the logical form
Recognize this as a modus tollens: if P then Q; not Q; therefore not P. No further premise is needed.
The argument is valid in its logical form; what matters is whether the consequent is truly false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : نقول : لو كان حكم العقل في التحسين والتقبيح معتبراً ، لصح من الله تكليف عباده بمعرفته ، وبالثناء عليه ، وبالشكر لنعمه . وهذا باطل ، فذاك باطل.
The fourth proof: we say: if the judgment of reason concerning good and evil were considered valid, then it would be valid from God to oblige His servants to know Him, and to praise Him, and to thank Him for His blessings. But this is invalid, so that is invalid.
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage presents a concise argument against the validity of rational judgments (al-tahsin wa al-taqbih) in God's actions. The author states that if such judgments were valid, then it would be permissible (or valid) for God to impose upon His servants the obligation to know Him, to praise Him, and to thank Him for His blessings. However, the author asserts that this conclusion is false (batil), and therefore the premise (the validity of rational judgment) is also false. The argument is a simple reductio: the consequence (obligation to know, praise, and thank) is deemed impossible or inappropriate for God to command (perhaps because it would impose a duty on God or because humans cannot fulfill it without God's grace), so the premise is rejected.

Key Terms
الحجة الرابعة
التحسين والتقبيح
تكليف
معرفة الله
الثناء
الشكر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that reason can independently determine what God must do, so God would be bound to reward good and punish evil.
Correction: This argument shows that if reason were that powerful, then God would be obligated to command certain duties—a consequence the author rejects as impossible, thus defending divine transcendence.

Checkpoint
What is the logical form of the fourth argument?
A) Modus ponens
B) Modus tollens
C) Affirming the consequent
D) Denying the antecedent

Key Takeaway
The fourth argument uses a simple reductio: if reason could judge good and evil, it would oblige God—an impossibility, so rational judgment is not valid for God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that God 'owes' you something—a prayer answered, a reward for good deeds? How does this argument expose that feeling as rooted in entitlement rather than the reality that God is free from any external obligation? What must you change in your heart today?
?
If reason cannot oblige God, then where does that leave your own attempts to justify your actions? Is there an area of your life where you are using your own logic to override God's command, thinking that He should accept it? What would it take for you to submit completely, even when your mind disagrees?
?
The argument concludes that rational judgments are invalid regarding God's obligations. Consider your own worship: Do you truly accept that God has no 'duty' to save you or forgive you? How does that truth change the way you approach Him—are you relying on His grace or on your own deserving? Make a specific commitment to a more humble posture in your next prayer.

Refutation of Rational Judgments by Infinite Regress
Refutation of the third division by infinite regress and establishment of divine free agency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how applying rational judgments of good and evil to God's actions leads to an infinite regress and the conclusion that the world would be eternal, thus refuting the validity of such judgments regarding God.

Core Idea
If rational good and evil applied to God, the world would be eternal, not created.

This passage presents a key argument in Islamic theology against the idea that human rational categories of good and evil can be used to judge God's actions. The author shows that if we assume God chose a specific time for creation for a rational reason, we fall into an infinite regress, forcing us to admit either purposelessness or the world's eternity. This undermines the very notion of a free creator.

Guided Path
1
Understand the division of possibilities
The passage first dismisses the second division (that the benefit is a necessary concomitant of the time) as already refuted. Focus on the third division: the time itself has a particular property not from itself or its concomitants.
The first two divisions are mentioned only to be set aside; the third is the target.
2
Follow the infinite regress
If the time is specified by a property not from itself, the free agent must have chosen that property. If that property were different, the same reasoning applies, leading to an infinite chain. This regress is impossible.
The author uses 'تسلسل' (infinite regress) as a logical impossibility.
3
Draw the conclusion about rational judgments
Assuming the world is created, the free agent must have chosen the time without any wisdom or benefit—sheer purposelessness (عبث), which is considered ugly by rational judgment. Therefore, if rational judgments were valid, the world would have to be eternal and God a necessary agent, not free.
This contradicts the doctrine of free agency, so rational judgments about God are invalid.
4
Apply the argument broadly
The author notes that this same reasoning applies to every event, attribute, measure, and location. It is not one proof but infinite proofs, as each originated thing involves a specific time and property.
This reinforces the universality of the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقسم الثاني : وهو أن المقتضي الحصول تلك المصلحة لازم من لوازم ذلك الوقت ، فهذا أيضاً باطل ، لعين تلك الدلائل الثلاثة .
وأما القسم الثالث : وهو أن ذلك الوقت اختص بتلك الخاصية لا لذاته ، ولا لشيء من لوازم ذاته ، فحينئذ وجب القطع بأن الفاعل المختار اختص ذلك الوقت بتلك الخاصية المعينة ، وإن كان ذلك الخاصية أخرى عاد التقسيم الأول فيه ، ولزم التسلسل ، وهو محال ، فيثبت أن بتقدير أن يكون العالم محدثاً فإنه يجب الاعتراف بأن الفاعل المختار خصص إحداثه بذلك الوقت المعين من غير رعاية حكمة ولا مصلحة ، فيكون ذلك محض العبث ، وهو قبيح في العقول ، فيثبت بهذا أن حكم العقل في التحسين والتقبيح لو كان معتبراً ، لزم القول بقدم العالم، ويكون الإله موجباً بالذات ، لا فاعلا مختاراً ، لكنا بينا : أن القول بالتحسين والتقبيح ، فرع على القول بإثبات الفاعل المختار ، وكل فرع أوجب فساد الأصل كان باطلاً ، فوجب أن يكون القول بالتحسين والتقبيح باطلاً في أحكام الله
واعلم . أن هذا الدليل الذي ذكرناه عائد في حدوث كل واحد من الحوادث في وقته المعين ، وفي اختصاص كل واحدة من الذوات بصفته المعينة ، وبمقداره المعين، وبحيزه المعين . فإنا نقول : اختصاصه بتلك الخاصية إما أن يكون الخاصية في ذلك الوقت ، أو لشيء من لوازم ذلك الوقت ، أو لشيء من عوارض ذلك الوقت ، وحينئذ يعود التقسيم بتمامه . وهذا الدليل في الحقيقة ليس دليلاً واحداً ، بل هو دلائل لا نهاية لها بحسب حدوث كل حادث يحدث إلى الأبد الذي لا آخر له
The second division: that the thing that necessitates the attainment of that benefit is a necessary concomitant of that time. This too is false, for the same three proofs. As for the third division: that that time is specified with that particular property not for itself and not for any concomitant of itself. Then it must be concluded that the free agent specified that time with that particular property. If that property were something else, the first division would return in it, and an infinite regress would be necessary, which is impossible. So it is established that, assuming the world is originated, it must be acknowledged that the free agent allocated its origination to that specific time without regard for any wisdom or benefit. Thus that would be sheer purposelessness, which is considered ugly by intellects. So it is established by this that if the judgment of the intellect concerning good and evil were considered valid, then the world would have to be considered eternal, and God would be a necessary agent by essence, not a free agent. But we have shown that the doctrine of good and evil is subsidiary to the affirmation of the free agent, and every subsidiary that necessitates the corruption of the principle is invalid. Therefore the doctrine of good and evil is necessarily invalid regarding God's judgments. Know that this argument we have mentioned applies to the origination of every single event at its specific time, and to the specification of every essence with its specific attribute, its specific measure, and its specific location. For we say: its specification with that property is either because the property is in that time, or because of a concomitant of that time, or because of an accident of that time. Then the entire division returns. And this argument is in reality not a single proof, but proofs without end according to the origination of every event that occurs forever without end.
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the third division of an argument about the specific time chosen by God for creation. It begins by dismissing the second division—that the benefit is a necessary concomitant of the time—as false due to the same three proofs already mentioned. The author then focuses on the third division: that the time itself has a particular property not from itself or its concomitants. If so, the free agent (الفاعل المختار) must have chosen that specific time. If the property were different, the first division would recur, leading to an infinite regress (تسلسل), which is impossible. Thus, assuming the world is originated, the free agent must have specified the time without any wisdom or benefit—this would be sheer purposelessness (عبث), which is considered ugly by rational judgment. This shows that if rational judgments of good and evil were valid, then the world would have to be eternal, and God would be a necessary agent by essence (موجباً بالذات), not a free agent. However, the author states that the doctrine of good and evil is subsidiary to the affirmation of the free agent—a subsidiary that destroys its principle is invalid, hence the doctrine of good and evil is invalid regarding God's actions. Finally, the author notes that this argument applies to every event and attribute, making it not just one proof but an infinite number of proofs.

Key Terms
الفاعل المختار
التحسين والتقبيح
التسلسل
العبث
موجباً بالذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's actions must have a rationally discernible purpose.
Correction: This passage shows that if rational judgments were applicable to God, an infinite regress results, making the world eternal and God a necessary agent. Thus, such judgments cannot be used for God's actions.

Checkpoint

A) The world would be eternal and God would be a free agent.
B) The world would be eternal and God would be a necessary agent by essence.
C) The world would be originated and God would be a free agent.
D) The world would be originated and God would be a necessary agent.

Key Takeaway
The application of rational good and evil to God's actions leads to an infinite regress, proving that such judgments are invalid and that God is a free agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever silently accused God of acting pointlessly when something bad happened in your life? After understanding the infinite regress argument, can you now submit to His free choice without demanding a rational justification? Will you truly let go of that complaint today?
?
This lesson shows that applying our sense of 'good' and 'evil' to God leads to logical absurdity. When you are tempted to declare something unfair in your life's circumstances, what specific argument from this passage will you recall to silence that pride? What will you say to yourself in that moment?
?
If rational judgments are invalid for God's actions, then your own sense of what is 'wise' or 'beneficial' may be a delusion. Are you willing to accept that your life's events, even the painful ones, come from a free Agent whose wisdom surpasses your categories? What would change in your heart right now if you truly believed that?

Rational Judgments

First Aspect of the Conditional: Three Premises Against Knowledge as Obligation
Author presents the first aspect of the conditional argument against reason's authority, supported by three premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first aspect of the conditional argument that shows reason's judgment is not authoritative over God's actions, and the three premises that support it.

Core Idea
God's commands are not subject to human reason's approval or disapproval.

In this passage, the author presents a challenging argument against those who claim that reason alone can determine the moral value of divine commands. He structures his argument as a conditional: if reason were authoritative, then commanding knowledge and obedience would be ugly; but it is not, so reason is not authoritative. To support this, he provides three premises that demonstrate the difficulty and lack of benefit in the obligation to know God. This lesson will walk through each premise and the overall argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional statement
Read the first paragraph of the passage carefully. Notice how the author states the conditional: if reason's judgment were valid, then commanding knowledge would be ugly, but it isn't, so reason is not authoritative.
This sets up the entire argument's structure.
2
Examine the first premise: difficulty of knowledge
The first premise argues that acquiring knowledge of God is extremely difficult, both because of the hard path of inference and the severe risk of eternal punishment for going astray. See how the author uses the fact that most people deviate as evidence.
3
Examine the second premise: God does not benefit
The second premise argues that God, being transcendent, does not derive any benefit from our thanks or worship. Contrast this with human gratitude where the benefactor feels joy.
4
Examine the third premise: we do not benefit
The third premise argues that we as worshippers also do not benefit from worship, because God can grant pleasure and remove pain directly without the need for obligations. Thus, the obligation is burdensome with no benefit.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Putting the three premises together, the author concludes that the obligation is ugly in reason, but since God commands it, reason's judgment is not valid over God. Reflect on the implications of this argument.
This is a reductio argument against rationalist ethics.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان الشرطية من وجهين :
الأول : إن تحصيل معرفة الله عمل شاق على العبد ، ولا يحصل منه نفع ، لا للشاكر ولا للمشكور ، وما كان كذلك كان الأمر به قبيحاً ، بمقتضى تحسين العقل وتقبيحه ينتج : أنه لو كان حكم عقولنا معتبراً في حق الله تعالى لوجب أن يقبح من الله أن يأمر عباده بمعرفته وطاعته
وهذا الكلام مبني على مقدمات :
المقدمة الأولى : في بيان أن تحصيل معرفة الله شاق : وبيانه من وجهين : الأول : لا شك أن تحصيل معرفة الله لا يتم إلا بالاستدلال ، ولا شك أن الاستدلال طريق صعب ، ولذلك فإن أكثر أهل العالم زاغوا وضلوا إلا القليل . ومن خاض في العلوم الإلهية علم ما فيها من الصعوبة والشدة . والثاني : إن بتقدير أن يضل الطالب بهذا العلم يستوجب العذاب العظيم الدائم ، ثم رأينا أن الأكثرين يضلون ويكفرون . وذلك يدل على أن الخطر شديد في هذا العلم.
المقدمة الثانية : في بيان أن المشكور لا ينتفع بهذا الشكر والطاعة : والأمر فيه ظاهر ، لأن المعبود متعالي عن النفع والضر ، والغم والسرور . وبهذا الحرف يظهر الفرق بين شكر المنعم في الشاهد، وبين شكره في حق الله تعالى . لأن الواحد منا إذا أنعم على غيره ، فإن أقدم المنعم عليه على شكر ذلك المنعم ، فرح المنعم بذلك الشكر ، وحصل في قلبه لذة عظيمة وسرور ، وإن أعرض عن شكره حصل في قلبه الحزن والغم والضرر ، ولما كانت هذه الأمور ممتنعة في حق الله تعالى ، فقد ظهر الفرق
المقدمة الثالثة : في بيان أن الشاكر لا ينتفع بهذا الشكر والعبادة : والدليل عليه : إنا قد بينا أنه لا معنى للمنفعة إلا اللذة والسرور ، أو دفع الألم والغم وما يكون وسيلة إلى أحد هذه الأشياء. ومن المعلوم بالضرورة أن القادر المختار قادر على تحصيل هذه الأمور من غير واسطة هذه التكاليف ولا يمكن أن يقال : إن تحصيل هذه الأشياء بواسطة التكاليف أيسر وأسهل ، لأنا نقول : إن حصول هذا التفاوت بالنسبة إلى قدرة الله محال ، فيثبت بهذه المقدمات الثلاثة : أن التكليف بالمعرفة والطاعة تكليف بأعمال شاقة، مع كونها خالية عن جميع جهات الفائدة ومثل هذا قبيح في العقل ، فلو كان حكم العقل معتبراً في أفعال الله وفي أحكامه ، لوجب أن يقبح من الله كل ذلك ، ولما لم يقبح منه شيء علمنا : أن حكم العقل غير معتبر في حقه
Explanation of the conditional from two aspects:
The first: Verily, acquiring knowledge of God is a difficult work for the servant, and no benefit is obtained from it, neither for the giver of thanks (al-shākir) nor for the object of thanks (al-mashkūr). And whatever is like that, commanding it is ugly (qabīḥ), according to the principle of reason’s approval and disapproval (taḥsīn al-ʿaql wa-taqbīḥihi). It results: that if the judgment of our intellects were considered valid in the right of God Almighty, then it would be necessary that it be deemed ugly from God that He command His servants to know Him and obey Him.
And this statement is based on premises:
The first premise: Concerning the explanation that acquiring knowledge of God is difficult: its explanation is from two aspects: The first: There is no doubt that acquiring knowledge of God is not completed except by inference (istidlāl), and there is no doubt that inference is a difficult path, and therefore most of the people of the world have deviated and gone astray except a few. And whoever delves into the divine sciences knows what is in them of difficulty and severity. The second: If it is supposed that the student goes astray through this science, he deserves eternal great punishment; then we see that the majority go astray and disbelieve. And that indicates that the danger is severe in this science.
The second premise: Concerning the explanation that the object of thanks (al-mashkūr) does not benefit from this thanks and obedience: The matter in it is apparent, because the worshipped is exalted above benefit and harm, and grief and joy. And by this point the difference appears between thanking the benefactor in the witnessed realm (al-shāhid) and thanking Him in the right of God Almighty. Because when one of us bestows a favor on another, if the recipient proceeds to thank that benefactor, the benefactor rejoices at that thanks, and a great pleasure and joy occur in his heart; and if he turns away from thanking him, grief, distress, and harm occur in his heart. And since these things are impossible in the right of God Almighty, the difference has become clear.
The third premise: Concerning the explanation that the giver of thanks (al-shākir) does not benefit from this thanks and worship: The proof for it: We have shown that there is no meaning of benefit except pleasure and joy, or repulsion of pain and grief, and whatever is a means to one of these things. And it is known by necessity that the powerful chooser (al-qādir al-mukhtār) is able to obtain these things without the mediation of these obligations (takālīf). And it cannot be said that obtaining these things through obligations is easier and simpler, because we say: The occurrence of this disparity with respect to God’s power is impossible. So it is established by these three premises: that the obligation of knowledge and obedience is an obligation with difficult works, along with their being devoid of all aspects of benefit. And like this is ugly in reason. So if the judgment of reason were considered valid in God’s actions and His rulings, then it would be necessary that all of that be deemed ugly from God. And since nothing is deemed ugly from Him, we know that the judgment of reason is not considered valid in His right.
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage presents the first aspect (al-wajh al-awwal) of a conditional argument (sharṭiyya) that aims to show that reason’s judgment is not authoritative in God’s affairs. The author begins by stating the conditional: if reason were authoritative, then commanding knowledge and obedience would be ugly, but it is not ugly, so reason is not authoritative. This conditional is then supported by three premises.

The first premise (al-muqaddima al-ūlā) argues that acquiring knowledge of God is difficult. The author gives two reasons: first, knowledge requires inference (istidlāl), which is a hard path, as seen by the majority of people going astray. Second, the risk of error is great, since going astray leads to eternal punishment, and most people indeed go astray. Thus the danger and difficulty are clear.

The second premise (al-muqaddima al-thāniya) argues that the object of thanks (al-mashkūr, i.e., God) does not benefit from our thanks. This is because God is transcendent above benefit and harm, joy and grief. In human experience (al-shāhid), when we thank a benefactor, the benefactor feels joy. But since God is above such emotions, our thanks gives Him no benefit. Therefore, the analogy with human gratitude fails.

The third premise (al-muqaddima al-thālitha) argues that the giver of thanks (al-shākir) also does not benefit from the act of worship. Benefit is defined as pleasure or removal of pain. But God, being all-powerful, can grant these directly without the need for religious obligations. It cannot be argued that obligations make it easier, because that would imply a disparity in God’s power, which is impossible.

From these three premises, the author concludes that the obligation to know and obey God is a burden with no benefit, which reason would deem ugly. Since God does command it, and it is not actually ugly (as God is wise), reason’s judgment cannot be authoritative over God. The argument is a reductio against those who claim that reason alone can determine the moral value of divine commands.

Key Terms
تحصيل معرفة الله
الشكر
الشاكر
المشكور
تحسين العقل وتقبيحه
التكليف
الاستدلال
الشاهد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author is denying the value of reason entirely.
Correction: The author is only denying reason's authority over God's actions, not rejecting reason's use in understanding religion or in other domains.

Checkpoint
According to the first premise, why is acquiring knowledge of God considered difficult?
A) Because it requires long study and many books
B) Because it requires inference and most people go astray
C) Because God makes it difficult intentionally
D) Because it involves physical hardship

Key Takeaway
The author argues that if human reason were authoritative over God, then commanding difficult acts with no benefit would be deemed ugly; since God does command such acts, reason is not authoritative.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a divine command that feels burdensome or unreasonable, do you catch yourself questioning God's wisdom? How does this lesson's argument that reason cannot judge God expose the pride behind that thought? Will you commit to submitting even when it makes no sense to you?
?
The author says God gains nothing from your worship. Does your prayer sometimes feel like you are doing God a favor? If so, how does this truth humble you? What would it mean for you to worship with absolute neediness, not as a giver but as a beggar?
?
If reason cannot judge God's actions, what happens when your mind rebels against a trial or a decree you don't understand? Will you trust His wisdom over your own judgment? What practical step will you take today to surrender that area of your life?

The Second Aspect: Noble vs. Base Benefactors
Author uses human analogy to argue that a noble benefactor does not demand service, unlike a base one, applying this to God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses a human analogy to argue that it is beneath God's majesty to demand worship in return for His favors.

Core Idea
A noble benefactor gives without demanding; a base one demands service. God is most noble.

The author presents a second angle to his argument by looking at human customs. He compares two types of benefactors: one who requires service in return for a favor is considered base and vengeful, while one who gives freely without expectation is noble. This human analogy then sets the stage to ask: how could it befit God, who is exalted above all human emotions, to demand anything from His creatures? The passage invites us to reflect on divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the full translation to grasp the flow.
The passage ends abruptly; note that it is part of a larger argument.
2
Identify the two types of benefactors
Find where the author describes the base benefactor (demands service, vengeful) and the noble benefactor (gives freely, no demand).
3
Contrast their characteristics
Note the stark difference: one is lowly, the other noble. Consider why the author uses such strong language.
The base benefactor's anger and revenge are highlighted.
4
Apply the analogy to God
Read the final sentence applying to God. Ask: why would the author conclude that demanding service is unbecoming of God?
God is transcendent above emotions like anger and joy, unlike humans.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني في بيان الشرطية : إن في الشاهد : من أنعم على بعض الضعفاء بنعمة ، فإن لم يكلفه في مقابلة تلك النعمة بالخدمة الشاقة كان موقع ذلك الإنعام شريفاً عالياً ، أما إن كلفه بأن يقابل تلك النعمة بالخدمة فالناس يقولون إنه لتيم دنيء الهمة ، يطلب في مقابلة تلك النعمة : الخدمة الشاقة المتعبة . بل نقول : إنه لا يرغب في هذا الطلب إلا من كان دنيء الهمة ، خسيس النفس ، يستعظم قدر تلك النعمة ، فيطالب المنعم عليه بأن يعطيه عوضاً على ذلك الإنعام ، فإن قصر المنعم عليه ، في أداء ذلك العوض ، وقع في قلبه غيظ وغضب وحقد ورغبة في الانتقام ، ثم يقع في العذاب الشديد. بسبب استيلاء هذه الصفات عليه ، ثم إنه يسعى في إيصال الضرر والألم إلى ذلك الشخص ليتوصل بذلك إلى الخلاص عن تلك الآلام الواقعة في قلبه ، بسبب استيلاء الغضب والغيظ عليه. ومثل هذا المنعم يكون قليل الخير ، كثير الضرر ، دنيء النفس ، ساقط الهمة . وأما إذا أنعم ، ولم يطلب من المنعم عليه شكراً وثناء ، ولا يؤذيه بسبب إقدامه على كفران تلك النعمة . فهذا يعد شريف النفس ، قوي الهمة ، عالي الدرجة ، هذا في حق العبد الذي يسره الشكر ، ويسوؤه الكفران. أما الإله المتعالي عن السيرور والغم والنفع والضرر ، فكيف يليق بجلاله وكبريائه، أن ينعم على عبده بنعمة ، ثم يطالبه
The second aspect concerning the explanation of the conditional: Verily, in the witnessed realm (al-shāhid): whoever bestows a favor upon some weak person with a favor, if he does not obligate him in exchange for that favor with difficult service, then the position of that favor is noble and high. But if he obligates him to correspond to that favor with service, then people say that he is a lowly, base-minded person who seeks, in exchange for that favor, difficult, tiring service. Rather, we say: No one desires this demand except one who is base-minded, vile of soul, who magnifies the value of that favor, and thus demands from the recipient that he give him a recompense for that favor. And if the recipient falls short in performing that recompense, then in his heart occur wrath, anger, hatred, and a desire for revenge, and then he falls into severe punishment due to these traits overwhelming him. Then he strives to bring harm and pain to that person in order to achieve relief from those pains occurring in his heart due to the predominance of anger and wrath upon him. And such a benefactor is of little good, much harm, base soul, fallen in aspiration. But if he bestows a favor and does not demand from the recipient thanks and praise, nor does he harm him due to his committing ingratitude for that favor, then this one is considered noble of soul, strong of aspiration, high in degree. This is with regard to the servant who is pleased by thanks and displeased by ingratitude. As for the God who is exalted above joy and grief, benefit and harm, how does it befit His majesty and grandeur that He bestow a favor upon His servant, then demand from him...
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage presents the second aspect (al-wajh al-thānī) of the conditional argument. The author uses an analogy from human custom (al-shāhid) to illustrate the nobility or baseness of demanding service in return for a favor. He begins by describing two types of benefactors in human experience.

First, if a person bestows a favor on a weak person and does not require difficult service in return, people consider that benefactor noble and high. Conversely, if he does require such service, people see him as lowly and base, one who magnifies the value of his own favor and demands payment. Such a person becomes angry and vengeful if not repaid, leading to severe inner turmoil and harm to others. This benefactor is described as of little good, much harm, and base aspiration.

Second, if a benefactor gives without demanding thanks or praise, and does not harm the recipient even if ungrateful, that benefactor is considered noble, strong in aspiration, and high in degree. But this evaluation applies only to human beings who are affected by gratitude or ingratitude—they feel joy from thanks and sorrow from ingratitude.

Now, the author applies this to God: God is absolutely transcendent (mutāʿāl) above joy and grief, benefit and harm. So how could it befit His majesty and grandeur to bestow a favor upon His servant and then demand something from him? The argument is cut off here, but the implied conclusion is that since demanding service in return for a favor is base in human custom, and God is far more noble than any human, it would be even more unworthy of God to demand service or worship in return for His favors. Therefore, the obligation to know and obey God cannot be based on reason’s expectation of reward or benefit, but rather on God’s command itself. The passage reinforces the idea that reason is not the measure of divine action.

Key Terms
الشاهد
الإنعام
الشكر
الهمة
الكفران
المتعالي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the author is denying that God demands anything, but he is arguing that reason cannot impose obligations on God based on human analogies.
Correction: The author is not denying God's right to demand worship; he is showing that if human nobility forbids demanding repayment, then God, who is infinitely more noble, cannot be conceived as demanding reciprocation for His favors. The obligation to worship comes from God's command, not from reason's expectation of reward.

Checkpoint
According to the second aspect, what is the characteristic of a benefactor who demands service in return for a favor?
A) Noble and high
B) Lowly, base-minded, and vengeful
C) Strong and generous
D) Forgiving and patient

Key Takeaway
Human custom shows that demanding repayment for a favor is base; therefore, it is unworthy of God to demand anything from His servants.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you help someone, do you ever catch yourself hoping for a 'thank you' or some recognition? How does this lesson's description of a 'noble benefactor' expose the state of your heart? Will you sincerely repent and try to give without expecting anything in return, even a simple thanks?
?
If God, the Most Noble, does not demand repayment for His favors, how should this change the way you view your acts of worship? Do you perform salah or give charity with a sense of obligation or as a grateful response? Right now, decide one concrete change in your intention for today's worship.
?
The author says a base benefactor becomes angry and vengeful when not repaid. Is there anyone in your life you resent because they didn't appreciate your kindness? Will you forgive them and release that anger today, seeking to be like the noble benefactor, and ultimately like God who gives even to the ungrateful?
